    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer16322573099171657775"><b>¿Cuál es el verdadero rostro del amor?</b><br/><br/>Ricardo ve cumplido, a una edad muy temprana, el sueño que en su Lima natal alimentó desde que tenía uso de razón: vivir en París. Pero el rencuentro con un amor de adolescencia lo cambiará todo. La joven, inconformista, aventurera, pragmática e inquieta, lo arrastrará fuera del pequeño mundo de sus ambiciones.<br/><br/>Testigos de épocas convul</span>
  <span id="freeText16322573099171657775" style="display:none"><b>¿Cuál es el verdadero rostro del amor?</b><br /><br />Ricardo ve cumplido, a una edad muy temprana, el sueño que en su Lima natal alimentó desde que tenía uso de razón: vivir en París. Pero el rencuentro con un amor de adolescencia lo cambiará todo. La joven, inconformista, aventurera, pragmática e inquieta, lo arrastrará fuera del pequeño mundo de sus ambiciones.<br /><br />Testigos de épocas convulsas y florecientes en ciudades como Londres, París, Tokio o Madrid, que aquí son mucho más que escenarios, ambos personajes verán sus vidas entrelazarse sin llegar a coincidir del todo. Sin embargo, esta danza de encuentros y desencuentros hará crecer la intensidad del relato página a página hasta propiciar una verdadera fusión del lector con el universo emocional de los protagonistas.<br /><br />Creando una admirable tensión entre lo cómico y lo trágico, Mario Vargas Llosa juega en Travesuras de la niña mala (2006) con la realidad y la ficción para liberar una historia en la que el amor se nos muestra indefinible, dueño de mil caras, como la niña mala. Pasión y distancia, azar y destino, dolor y disfrute... ¿Cuál es el verdadero rostro del amor?</span>
  <a data-text-id="16322573099171657775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1618421-hello-community-can-you-recommend-me-a">Hello community. Can you recommend me a next book of Mario Vargas Lllosa? I have a serious challenge. The &#39;Conversación en La Catedra&#39; was too difficult for me. I was not able to process the switching time and context and gave up after 200 pages. The &#39;Travesuras de la niña mala&#39; was the opposite (in my own experience). It was nearly too simple but for the most so &#39;Harlequin&#39; type. Which one would be in between?</a>
</div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1932350095?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Finocchiaro" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro">Michael Finocchiaro</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1932350095" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16948497552434257179">Where can I even start? This book was so excellent, I nearly cried - twice! Seriously! I mean, I did my research: I reread Madame Bovary and then MVL's excellent critique in The Perpetual Orgy before his homage to Flaubert, The Bad Girl. But even this did not prepare me for the depth of emotion and sentiment expressed in this MVL masterpiece. Perhaps it is because the narrator Ricardo, like me, is a Parisian ex-pat, I was enamored with his descriptions of life here (perfectly and clinically accu</span>
  <span id="freeText16948497552434257179" style="display:none">Where can I even start? This book was so excellent, I nearly cried - twice! Seriously! I mean, I did my research: I reread Madame Bovary and then MVL's excellent critique in The Perpetual Orgy before his homage to Flaubert, The Bad Girl. But even this did not prepare me for the depth of emotion and sentiment expressed in this MVL masterpiece. Perhaps it is because the narrator Ricardo, like me, is a Parisian ex-pat, I was enamored with his descriptions of life here (perfectly and clinically accurate) or because I am deep down a hopeless romantic, but this book, the impossible love between the "good boy" and the "bad girl" just blew me away. Every chapter presents a separate encounter between these protagonists - substitues for Emma and Léon from Bovary - and each are so beautifully drawn with delicious detail. It is pure reading ecstasy. I think that this may be my favorite MvL book or at worst in the top 2 or 3. Gorgeous, moving, heartrending - as real literature's mission is to be, this is a masterpiece and a must read.<br />Even after finishing all of MVL's translated novels, this one is still one of my top 3!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7351608422447400900"><b>Travesuras de la Niña Mala = Tours et détours de la Vilaine Fille = The Bad Girl, Mario Vargas Llosa</b><br/><br/>Characters: Ricardo Somocurcio, La niña mala, Paúl, Juan Barreto, Los Gravoski<br/><br/>The Bad Girl, originally published in 2006 in Spanish as Travesuras de la niña mala, is a novel by Peruvian author Mario Vargas Llosa, who won the Nobel Prize in Literature in 2010. <br/><br/>Journalist Kathryn Harrison approvingly argues that the book is a rewrite (rather than simply a recycling) of the French realist Gustave Fla</span>
  <span id="freeText7351608422447400900" style="display:none"><b>Travesuras de la Niña Mala = Tours et détours de la Vilaine Fille = The Bad Girl, Mario Vargas Llosa</b><br /><br />Characters: Ricardo Somocurcio, La niña mala, Paúl, Juan Barreto, Los Gravoski<br /><br />The Bad Girl, originally published in 2006 in Spanish as Travesuras de la niña mala, is a novel by Peruvian author Mario Vargas Llosa, who won the Nobel Prize in Literature in 2010. <br /><br />Journalist Kathryn Harrison approvingly argues that the book is a rewrite (rather than simply a recycling) of the French realist Gustave Flaubert's classic novel Madame Bovary (1856). In Vargas Llosa's version, the plot relates the decades-long obsession of its narrator, a Peruvian expatriate, with a woman with whom he first fell in love when they were both teenagers.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز هجدهم ماه نوامبر سال 2010میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: دختری از پرو؛ نویسنده: ماریو وارگاس یوسا؛ مترجم خجسته کیهان؛ تهران، پارسه، 1388؛ در 352ص؛ شابک 9786005026696؛ چاپ ششم 1392؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان پرویی - سده 20م<br /><br />داستان زندگی «ریکاردو»، و عاشقی او به یک دختر پرویی است؛ «دختری از پرو» نوشته «ماریو بارگاس یوسا»، نویسنده «پرویی» و یک رمان عاشاقانه از ادبیات آمریکای لاتین است؛ داستان درباره شخصیتی به نام «ریکاردو» است، که در «شیلی» با دختری روبرو می‌شود، که او را «شیلیایی کوچک» می‌نامد؛ آن دختر بعدها زندگی ماجراجویانه، و پرفراز و نشیبی را در پیش می‌گیرد، و «ریکاردو» می‌کوشد، او را دنبال کند؛ این رمان آخرین رمان «یوسا»، پیش از آن است که جایزه ی نوبل ادبیات را از آن خود کند<br /><br />نقل نمونه ای از متن: (اما در واقع چیزی که آن تابستان را متمایز می‌کرد، حضور ناگهانی دو خواهر در «میافلورس» بود‌؛ دخترانی با ظاهر پر زرق و برق و بحث‌ انگیز، که از کشور دوردست «شیلی» آمده بودند؛ آن‌ها که تند و تند حرف می‌زدند، به گونه‌ ای تقلید‌ ناپذیر، کلمات را با «پ ف ا و ه» به پایان می‌رساندند، که به آه کشیدن، یا اظهار تعجب شبیه بود، و دل همه‌ ی ما را می‌برد؛ ما که به تازگی شلوار‌های کوتاه را کنار گذاشته، مثل بزرگ‌تر‌ها شلوار‌های بلند می‌پوشیدیم، و مرا شیفته‌ تر از دیگران می‌کرد؛ دو خواهر شبیه یکدیگر بودند؛ آنکه بزرگ‌تر بود، «لیلی» نام داشت، و کمی از دیگری که یکسال از او کوچک‌تر بود، و «لوسی» نامیده می‌شد، کوتاه‌ قدتر بود؛ «لیلی» بیش از چهارده پانزده سال نداشت، و «لوسی» سیزده چهارده ساله بود؛ انگار صفت «پُر زرق و برق» را برای آن‌ها ساخته بودند، با این حال «لوسی» کم‌تر از خواهرش، جلب توجه می‌کرد، نه تنها به این خاطر که مو‌های کوتاه‌ترش به بوری مو‌های او نبود، و نسبت به او ساده‌ تر لباس می‌پوشید، بلکه برای این‌که ساکت‌تر بود.؛ هنگام رقص نیز، اگرچه چنان قر می‌داد، که هیچیک از دختران «میافلورس» جرأت چنان حرکاتی را نداشت، ولی در مقایسه با لیلیِ آتشپاره، آن عروسک چرخان، آن شراره‌ ی مانده در باد، همچنان جدی‌تر، پر عقده و تقریباً بی‌مزه به نظر می‌آمد؛ لیلی به‌ محض این‌که صفحه‌ ی «مامبو» روی گرامافون به چرخش درمی‌آمد، می‌پرید وسط پیست؛ ...؛) پایان نقل.؛ <br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 13/08/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1403965203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11614206674728512528">How do you write a review of a book by an undoubtedly very talented writer that you found - mediocre?<br/><br/>I am not even going to try. <br/><br/>It was my fourth novel by Mario Vargas Llosa, and I will happily admit that he is a great storyteller. Only I found the story he told here about a "bad girl" to be quite pointless, a male fantasy told a thousand times already. As a study of sexual desire, I found it shallow and one-sided, and focused too much on superficial stimulation - and I found myself thinking rep</span>
  <span id="freeText11614206674728512528" style="display:none">How do you write a review of a book by an undoubtedly very talented writer that you found - mediocre?<br /><br />I am not even going to try. <br /><br />It was my fourth novel by Mario Vargas Llosa, and I will happily admit that he is a great storyteller. Only I found the story he told here about a "bad girl" to be quite pointless, a male fantasy told a thousand times already. As a study of sexual desire, I found it shallow and one-sided, and focused too much on superficial stimulation - and I found myself thinking repeatedly that it was an effect-seeking movie turned into a novel rather than an independent work of fiction. Sequence after sequence, we follow the pattern of exploring "good" and "bad" sexuality. Less would have been more exciting, I believe.<br /><br />I don't want to discourage anyone from reading the novel, as I think it is a matter of personal taste whether you like it or not - it just happened not to be my cup of tea at all.<br /><br />Moving on!</span>

<div id="review_132676263" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/132676263" />
    <a title="Seth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4351439-seth"><img alt="Seth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319242813p2/4351439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/132676263?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seth" href="/user/show/4351439-seth">Seth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427764" href="/book/show/4838360-the-bad-girl">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4351439-seth?shelf=latin-american-literature">latin-american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4351439-seth?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer132676263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5984875722415027819">This is the third and most fascinating book I have read by Mario Vargas Llosa. The others were "War of the End of the World" and "Death in the Andes." After reading this relatively modest sample of this literary giant’s oeuvre, I am already certain that he deserved the Nobel Prize for Literature far more than many other recipients. <br/><br/>Vargas Llosa is noteworthy not just as a prolific author but also for his political odyssey. Incidentally, "The Bad Girl" includes some trenchant insights into politi</span>
  <span id="freeText5984875722415027819" style="display:none">This is the third and most fascinating book I have read by Mario Vargas Llosa. The others were "War of the End of the World" and "Death in the Andes." After reading this relatively modest sample of this literary giant’s oeuvre, I am already certain that he deserved the Nobel Prize for Literature far more than many other recipients. <br /><br />Vargas Llosa is noteworthy not just as a prolific author but also for his political odyssey. Incidentally, "The Bad Girl" includes some trenchant insights into politics in his native Peru. Subsequent events have confirmed the author's fears about the impact of the looming rise of Alan Garcia on Peru’s economy. But this book is not about politics nor is it only about Peru. It is a parable akin to the flight of Icarus. It is about a woman who dares to escape her humble origins—whatever the risks and whoever may be harmed along the way. The philosophical questions posed by the book are: is the main character really a bad girl or is she a victim? Is she cruel or merely a survivor? Does she have delusions of grandeur or just ambition? Does she deserve our contempt or our pity?<br /><br />I will refrain from providing a plot summary of this globe-spanning adventure because that would ruin the experience for you. Therefore, I will confine my remarks to a more mundane aspect of the novel. The narrator, who works as a translator and interpreter for UNESCO, has occasion to contemplate the meaning of his profession—that is, when he is not chasing bad girl. His mentor counsels him that a translator/interpreter must convey other people’s ideas without introducing his own, thereby leaving no trace upon death. Therefore, the translator is nothing but a phantom. A sobering thought, indeed.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5984875722415027819" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17623712266528370445">Aahlmost second-rate. How many novels are better than this, one of MVL’s most recent? ah Many. All the knowledge of literary techniques and an impressive mastery of plot, character, mood, etc. does nothing for Mr. Vargas Llosa here. <br/><br/>The story is full of clichés (though it IS probably his most accessible) and the main character is a dullard. The plot is one we’ve heard before: girl drags boy along, in this case for an entire half-century, teasing him and lying, and basically treating him like shi</span>
  <span id="freeText17623712266528370445" style="display:none">Aahlmost second-rate. How many novels are better than this, one of MVL’s most recent? ah Many. All the knowledge of literary techniques and an impressive mastery of plot, character, mood, etc. does nothing for Mr. Vargas Llosa here. <br /><br />The story is full of clichés (though it IS probably his most accessible) and the main character is a dullard. The plot is one we’ve heard before: girl drags boy along, in this case for an entire half-century, teasing him and lying, and basically treating him like shit. “Great Expectations” is the only one of these “impossible love stories” that I can digest. This stuff is getting really old… a narrator (oops! Ricardito is supposedly a doppelganger of the writer himself!) who goes through the motions of a semi-charmed kind of life, traveling all over the globe, and anchored by the stupid magnetism of a vicious femme... Gag me with a spoon!</span>

<div id="review_125540696" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125540696" />
    <a title="Niledaughter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3521888-niledaughter"><img alt="Niledaughter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1270079734p2/3521888.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125540696?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Niledaughter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Niledaughter" href="/user/show/3521888-niledaughter">Niledaughter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571234110" href="/book/show/6535521-the-bad-girl">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Niledaughter by: </span>
      Mashael
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3521888-niledaughter?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3521888-niledaughter?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125540696" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18185938126022371079">I kept struggling few days to be able to write a review about this disturbing and enjoyable novel , it was my first read for Llosa and it will not be the last :)<br/>the novel is haunting /page turner whether you agree with its thoughts or not. Some critics linked this novel strongly to Llosa’s <a href='http://www.nytimes.com/2007/10/14/books/review/Harrison.html?pagewanted=all' rel='nofollow noopener'>obsession</a> with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2175.Madame_Bovary' title='Madame Bovary by Gustave Flaubert' rel='nofollow noopener'>Madame Bovary</a> as we briefly discussed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/676506?order=a&page=2' rel='nofollow noopener'>starting from post 61</a> , but this is not what I am going to discuss in here ; There are other several items to talk about reviewing this novel :<br/><br/>*For the main</span>
  <span id="freeText18185938126022371079" style="display:none">I kept struggling few days to be able to write a review about this disturbing and enjoyable novel , it was my first read for Llosa and it will not be the last :)<br />the novel is haunting /page turner whether you agree with its thoughts or not. Some critics linked this novel strongly to Llosa’s <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2007/10/14/books/review/Harrison.html?pagewanted=all" rel="nofollow noopener">obsession</a> with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2175.Madame_Bovary" title="Madame Bovary by Gustave Flaubert" rel="nofollow noopener">Madame Bovary</a> as we briefly discussed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/676506?order=a&amp;page=2" rel="nofollow noopener">starting from post 61</a> , but this is not what I am going to discuss in here ; There are other several items to talk about reviewing this novel :<br /><br />*For the main story “ Ricardo and the bad girl “ journey …<br /><br />Is this a love story? … May be I felt it that way at some points ...there is passion and sexual obsession but it is not a romantic novel any way . <br /><br />The protagonist (the good boy / Ricardo) doesn’t have a significant personality; yet the reader can never got bored by his single voice narration nor loose empathy with him . The heroin (the bad girl with like thousand names) was a cruel selfish even psycho figure ; she is as described <i>” can't be admired and can't, except in Ricardo's desperate parody of an old movie to be the heroine of a grand fatal romance.” </i> - yet I couldn’t completly hate her and I even pitied her at certain moments!<br /> <br />Starting with their adolescence we accompanied these two in a forty years journey, they meet and separate ... maintain an unusual relationship ; most of the time she is “the sadist” and he is “the masochist” ; they are friends ... they fight and clash ; they have their Reconciliation and peace ; as a whole they have their poignant times and warm times . all the time they can both see each other very clearly. They are both dreamers, Ricardo in an incurable romantic way, and she is self destructive ... chasing a fantastical world where she can gather all kind of pleasures in her possession.<br /><br /><br />The journey in each stop keep us anticipating ; and it sails in time and place amazingly , we discover with them places and history of the second half of the twentieth century, from Peru to Paris to London to Tokyo to Madrid and even Egypt’s Alexandria , each time we move to a new city we wear Llosa ‘s eyes to observe and discover the social and cultural mainstream of the day ; that lead to a comment I read/liked some where about the bad girl : <i>” she begins to look like an emblem of the later part of the 20th century, as seen from the point of view of a bewildered Peruvian“</i> !<br /><br /> <br />* politics:<br />As I read that Llosa is a writer whose deeply political work vividly examines the perils of power and corruption in Latin America. can this be sensed from one novel ? Yes ; there was so much politics in this one, Peru was always there, at the beginning and end as place to host the story, and as news and comments/ analysis all along the novel. It was not only Peru; we passed by Vietnam war and Africa ; there was bitterness all along . <br /><br /><br />* Charming side characters :<br />The novel takes place in seven chapters, each serves change in place and time line and in the same time bring us a new main / side character that would steal the light from the protagonists for a while ...from the revolutionist Peruvian , to the Hebei one , to the Interpreter Salomon “the dragoman”, the mute vitamin child , to the beautiful Marcella …Weaving the main story with these surrounders gave subtle progress to the novel .I loved the meditations specially about identity and belonging that Floated softly through the journey .<br /><br />Wow , I talked too much ! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18185938126022371079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_591881056" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/591881056" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/591881056?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer591881056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17434053316422496705"><i>The Bad Girl</i> is a lifelong love story - the tale of meetings and partings and a sad tale of corruption and deterioration.<br/><b>Mario Vargas Llosa</b> said once: "Eroticism has its own moral justification because it says that pleasure is enough for me; it is a statement of the individual's sovereignty."<br/>And <i>The Bad Girl</i> is a kind of illustration to this idea.<br/></span>

<div id="review_974244136" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/974244136" />
    <a title="Jackson Burnett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10106571-jackson-burnett"><img alt="Jackson Burnett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455878630p2/10106571.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/974244136?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jackson Burnett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jackson Burnett" href="/user/show/10106571-jackson-burnett">Jackson Burnett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427764" href="/book/show/4838360-the-bad-girl">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10106571-jackson-burnett?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10106571-jackson-burnett?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer974244136" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11727549750056597481">How do you have a love story without passion? How do you have a tale of obsession without palpable madness?<br/><br/>Mario Vargas Llosa always writes thoughtful novels, but the fiction of <i>The Bad Girl</i> needs visceral emotion. This novel disappoints in its absence.<br/><br/>Young Peruvian Ricardo Somocurcio falls in love with the mysterious Chilean girl Lily. Is she really Chilean? Who is she? The sentiment is not returned. She disappears. Ricardo grows into adulthood and moves to Paris where he works as a translator</span>
  <span id="freeText11727549750056597481" style="display:none">How do you have a love story without passion? How do you have a tale of obsession without palpable madness?<br /><br />Mario Vargas Llosa always writes thoughtful novels, but the fiction of <i>The Bad Girl</i> needs visceral emotion. This novel disappoints in its absence.<br /><br />Young Peruvian Ricardo Somocurcio falls in love with the mysterious Chilean girl Lily. Is she really Chilean? Who is she? The sentiment is not returned. She disappears. Ricardo grows into adulthood and moves to Paris where he works as a translator/interpreter. The mysterious girl, now a young woman, appears again in his life. His love is rekindled but not returned. She disappears once more and returns later with a different identity, and so the story goes for the next forty years. <br /><br />The bad girl is an intriguing character, but ultimately predictable. Her motivation, one dimensional. The novel is heavy with Forest Gumpian tropes and the ending, a contrived disappointment. Mario Vargas Llosa has written better.<br /><br />Ricardo compares being a interpreter with being a phantom. One is present without being present, one exists without existing.<br /><br />Maybe that is the real tragedy here. Being a phantom means the love of your life can never be more than a phantom either.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11727549750056597481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2115680134" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2115680134" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2115680134?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427764" href="/book/show/4838360-the-bad-girl">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">bad girls and good boys</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=hot-sex">hot-sex</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=farts">farts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2115680134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13391724101627246626">You ever play kick the can when you were a kid? The way we used to play it, there was home base, and you would go running out to try to kick the can over, and the can is guarded, and if you made it back to home you couldn't get tagged.<br/><br/>It was all very metaphorical, obviously. The can is husbands. The guards are life. Home base is Ricardo, Ricardino, the little pissant narrator of this book. And you are a bad girl.<br/><br/>The bad girl captivates Ricardo when they're teenagers in Miraflores, Peru, and here</span>
  <span id="freeText13391724101627246626" style="display:none">You ever play kick the can when you were a kid? The way we used to play it, there was home base, and you would go running out to try to kick the can over, and the can is guarded, and if you made it back to home you couldn't get tagged.<br><br>It was all very metaphorical, obviously. The can is husbands. The guards are life. Home base is Ricardo, Ricardino, the little pissant narrator of this book. And you are a bad girl.<br><br>The bad girl captivates Ricardo when they're teenagers in Miraflores, Peru, and here's Miraflores by the way because it's very pretty:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1505221638i/23902653._SX540_.jpg" alt="mirflores" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>It's a neighborhood of Lima</i><br><br>And then they both go their ways. Ricardo goes off to live his dream, in Paris - and also London, Tokyo, Madrid - as a translator of conferences and literature. The bad girl goes to adventure. She's an adventuress, an <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2065268014" title="My review of Edith Wharton's Custom of the Country" rel="nofollow noopener">Undine Spragg,</a> always looking for her next, best husband. (She also owes a debt to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124515646" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Emma Bovary,</a> and that's apparently who Llosa was thinking about most as he wrote this.) She returns to Ricardo again and again, as each adventure ends for her, to marshal strength for the next one, and then re-abandons him.<br><br>The book, one of the most recent and popular by the wonderful, Nobel-winning Peruvian Mario Vargas Llosa, tracks them through their entire lives, as the bad girl periodically Miley Cyruses him.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1505221797ra/23902715.gif" alt="wrecking_ball" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>comes in like a wrecking ball</i><br><br>The effect is somewhat episodic, especially because in each chapter Llosa tells the entire life story of one of Ricardo's friends. It starts to feel like one of those dreaded "interconnected short story" deals. It's not too bad - Ricardo and the bad girl are so compelling that you have plenty of plot to pull you from one episode to the next - but it's there. It's my second Llosa, and I still like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143334444" title="My review of War of the End of the World" rel="nofollow noopener">the other one</a> better.<br><br>But it's very good, savage and tragic and sometimes hot. The bad girl is amazing, battered and tossed but never quitting on her totally amoral goals. You almost hope she gets that can.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13391724101627246626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/80763138?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780374182434" href="/book/show/615208.The_Bad_Girl">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=peruvian">peruvian</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer80763138" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5065463382095449966">In the hands of a lesser writer, this novel of obsessive love would be cheesey and unbelievable. But Vargas Llosa is a masterful storyteller. The story is told in the first person. The Bad Girl, who our narrator meets as a teen, returns again and again in his life, each time to capture his soul before shattering him with a virulent escape. This is a wonderful book, examining language, culture and psychology through the mind of a kind, intelligent man, stripped defenseless by love, or something l</span>
  <span id="freeText5065463382095449966" style="display:none">In the hands of a lesser writer, this novel of obsessive love would be cheesey and unbelievable. But Vargas Llosa is a masterful storyteller. The story is told in the first person. The Bad Girl, who our narrator meets as a teen, returns again and again in his life, each time to capture his soul before shattering him with a virulent escape. This is a wonderful book, examining language, culture and psychology through the mind of a kind, intelligent man, stripped defenseless by love, or something like love. The writing is earthy, candid and surprisingly hopeful. The novel ends in satisfying, sublime tenderness.<br /><br />I've noticed, through four of his novels, that Vargas Llosa can speak with many voices but always intelligently, always believably. It is nice to know he is prolific, so I look forward to the rest of his catalogue.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3140102901700883467"> The main character, Ricardo has always been in love with the "bad girl"; she is his obsession. His feelings for the bad girl have taken him across the world and back only to have his heart broken over and over again. He manages to overcome his feelings for sometime but always returns with an open heart.<br/><br/> The theme of my book is that you cannot buy happiness, no matter how much money or power your lover has, if you do not truly care for the person- you will not be happy.<br/><br/> When I think about the st</span>
  <span id="freeText3140102901700883467" style="display:none"> The main character, Ricardo has always been in love with the "bad girl"; she is his obsession. His feelings for the bad girl have taken him across the world and back only to have his heart broken over and over again. He manages to overcome his feelings for sometime but always returns with an open heart.<br /><br /> The theme of my book is that you cannot buy happiness, no matter how much money or power your lover has, if you do not truly care for the person- you will not be happy.<br /><br /> When I think about the story of the "good boy", Ricardo Somocurcio, and the "bad girl" my mind cannot help but think of Akon's song Dangerous. This song describes how dangerous the feelings of infatuation can be on a male. The lyrics accurately describe the effect that the bad girl has on Ricardo.<br /><br /> This book has many twist and turns that leave you wanting more. It is surreal what Ricardo puts himself through for the love of a girl who does not want to be with him. This is one of the most enjoyable books I have ever read, it captures the reader with every line and detail, you have to read it to know what I mean. </span>

<div id="review_1466719957" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1466719957" />
    <a title="Simon Hollway" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27637045-simon-hollway"><img alt="Simon Hollway" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1426912194p2/27637045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1466719957?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon Hollway" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon Hollway" href="/user/show/27637045-simon-hollway">Simon Hollway</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780374182434" href="/book/show/615208.The_Bad_Girl">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27637045-simon-hollway?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1466719957" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13190472399792387413">Latin lovers are by definition intense, spontaneous, operatic and, frustratingly, erratic. And so it was that Bolano seduced me first with ‘Savage Detectives’, disappointed me with most of his half-baked early offerings and then, the voluptuous promise of 2666, consummately failed to deliver on its swarthy, big-hipped swagger. Devastating.<br/><br/>Vargas Llosa was the obvious rebound date and he came on strong. ‘Conversation in the Cathedral’, ‘War of the End of the World’, ‘The Feast of the Goat’ - knee</span>
  <span id="freeText13190472399792387413" style="display:none">Latin lovers are by definition intense, spontaneous, operatic and, frustratingly, erratic. And so it was that Bolano seduced me first with ‘Savage Detectives’, disappointed me with most of his half-baked early offerings and then, the voluptuous promise of 2666, consummately failed to deliver on its swarthy, big-hipped swagger. Devastating.<br /><br />Vargas Llosa was the obvious rebound date and he came on strong. ‘Conversation in the Cathedral’, ‘War of the End of the World’, ‘The Feast of the Goat’ - knee-tremblers all. It was too good to be true. I became nervous. I started to doubt him. I doubted myself. This much was enough but I had to have more. <br /><br />Timidly, I snuck into ‘Aunt Julia and the Scriptwriter’. Genius. Feeling more secure, I delved into Vargas’ supposedly midweight excursions, starting with ‘Death in the Andes’. Masterful. Succulent. Devilish. The honeymoon had to end sometime, surely? <br /><br />How about his first book? ‘Time of the Hero’? Turned out to be my favourite -possibly the best first novel by any novelist ever in the history of words and that includes ‘The Wasp Factory’, ‘Pickwick Papers’ and ‘Genesis’ (the non-fiction religiousy thing not the computer game). A finer debut even than Tom Daley’s first inverted pike rip entry at the Olympics and the aching camera close-up on his Sammy Shammy as he wiped himself down, poolside.<br /><br />I needed to go cold turkey. I had to shake this obsession and gain some perspective. I opted for the badly reviewed ‘Dream of the Celt’ to smother my compulsion. But no. It was word perfect: a masterclass in sensitive historical research that performed sensuous mouth to mouth on an obscure, dead anti-hero.<br /><br />Then there was ‘Bad Girl’. One of the most highly rated turnouts of his more recent crop. I’d been tip-toeing round it for months. I didn’t like the name nor the clichéd cover art. The stockinged leg and the nothing title all felt a bit nelly naff.<br /><br />From the off, there was something very index-cardy about this book. First we have the default Peruvian childhood descriptors, assorted early-starter cloud and sun cameos scattered about, lukewarm splashes of exotic South American flora and fauna adding sterile scent and nostalgia to a sloppy backstory.<br /><br />Then we jump a couple of decades into the quasi-historical chapters – hello, picture postcard Paris. A croissant here, a bistro and half-hearted, under the pump kitchenhand there (‘Down and Out in Paris’ this ain’t). Some early characters fleshed out and summarily dispatched.<br /><br />Next stop, London in the swinging 60s. More characters. More random evictions.<br /><br />And so it goes. Another elephantine chapter begins, another handful of characters are wheeled out and then permanently plucked from sight. Nothing sticks. Nothing is sustained. No continuity apart from the inconstant flourishes of the bad girl who pops-up momentarily in the most preposterously coincidental and far-flung locations for a studiously dispassionate interlude before exiting stage right. She rambles aimlessly from one tableau to the other, making brief, low impact, stultifying appearances in each.<br /><br />The only glue holding it together is the anaemic, limp-minded protagonist whose insipid, prostate-challenged agency painfully drips with demi-emotions sketched in shorthand but never distilled. There’s no fragrance to the damned thing. Our ‘hero’ has as much life and sex appeal as a squashed grape or a conservative MP.<br /><br />Quite simply the narrative fails to flow or gel or THROB with Vargas’ usual hot-blooded exchanges. It feels formulaic. It bleats in sketchy, antiseptic vignettes, under-marinated in local history and culture. A hot and cold pickled smorgasbord. More a Scandinavian slide show populated by a hotch-potch of historical soundbites than his typical Latin American melodrama vibrating with frenzied filth.<br /><br />When the naughty lady suddenly materialises in Japan halfway through, I had to close the book. <br /><br />Travesty! <br /><br />A trial separation is now being negotiated. I may have to rekindle the relationship by reverting to his nascent twink twitterings. ‘The Green House’ anyone?<br /><br />If you adore Llosa, I would seriously recommend you avoid this particular trip until you’ve exhausted all other avenues and sucked the marrow out of his carcass. <br /><br />For lesser authors, this is a three star. For Llosa, a clanging two.<br /><br />Bad Girl and bad, bad Mario Vargas Llosa.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13190472399792387413" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_12987287" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12987287" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/398968-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361577733p2/398968.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12987287?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/398968-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12987287" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6419037475000275373">I became completely mesmerized by this book of obsessive love. The bad girl is a Peruvian girl the narrator knew when he was a boy, and she keeps coming in and out of his life in different identities. The more cruel she is to him, the more he loves her. But this intelligent, articulate, sensitive man is powerless to his obsession. He tries repeatedly to free himself from her spell, but he can't. He can't explain her power over him. I find that fascinating, haunting, and sad. She became the centr</span>
  <span id="freeText6419037475000275373" style="display:none">I became completely mesmerized by this book of obsessive love. The bad girl is a Peruvian girl the narrator knew when he was a boy, and she keeps coming in and out of his life in different identities. The more cruel she is to him, the more he loves her. But this intelligent, articulate, sensitive man is powerless to his obsession. He tries repeatedly to free himself from her spell, but he can't. He can't explain her power over him. I find that fascinating, haunting, and sad. She became the central story of his entire life. Whoever compared this book to Madame Bovary, well, it's nothing like Madame Bovary. The bad girl is a unique character in literature. I've never read anything like her. Wait a minute. I know! She's like Evita Peron. I loved the playful teasing between the narrator and the bad girl. You could feel the erotic tension between them, and at the same time you're screaming for him not to give in to her again, you somehow completely understand when he does. The writing is so strong, and you're pulled through the details of the decades in settings like Paris, London, Peru, Madrid, Tokyo. In short, this book is highly seductive.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6419037475000275373" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer225982477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16235567850223069763"> I’m going to have to say that I liked this book. The “good boy” upset me quit a bit in this book for putting up with so much shit from “the bad girl” but I still enjoyed it very much. <br/>	Besides being a love story, this is also a story about identity and love for your country…The author struggles with his memories/feelings from his childhood in Peru and his life in Europe. Mario Vargas Llosa has a way with words. I really liked his storytelling. He describes places and scenes in a way that makes </span>
  <span id="freeText16235567850223069763" style="display:none"> I’m going to have to say that I liked this book. The “good boy” upset me quit a bit in this book for putting up with so much shit from “the bad girl” but I still enjoyed it very much. <br />	Besides being a love story, this is also a story about identity and love for your country…The author struggles with his memories/feelings from his childhood in Peru and his life in Europe. Mario Vargas Llosa has a way with words. I really liked his storytelling. He describes places and scenes in a way that makes you feel you are there. I really wish I had read this in Spanish but this was a going-out-of-sale buy from borders (Spanish was not available). <br />	The book is about Ricardo Simorcucio’s life from his teenage years through his fifties and how this woman that he calls “the bad girl” pops in and out of his life. She is his vice, his addiction, he is obsessed with her, but she is a total bitch to him. I’m not sure if this is a love story because I would think a love story is two sided. A love story would be two people that are in love with each other, not just two people that feel some kind of love for each other. This book has a lot of happiness and a lot of sadness as well. It’s an emotional rollercoaster. <br /></span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1939157532?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elham" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elham" href="/user/show/43665577-elham">Elham</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1939157532" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15461792085348224786"><i>The Bad Girl</i> is like a biography, a story of a life; life of Ricardo Somocurcio, a man from Peru who in his life his ultimate goal was to live in Paris. He did. He worked as a translator in UNESCO and lived in an apartment in Joseph Granier Street. He wasn’t interested in politics that much to join one of those Communist parties nor was he much interested in those Hippie cults fashionable in 60’s. In fact he always kept his safety distance with everything. Except for love. He is badly in love wi</span>
  <span id="freeText15461792085348224786" style="display:none"><i>The Bad Girl</i> is like a biography, a story of a life; life of Ricardo Somocurcio, a man from Peru who in his life his ultimate goal was to live in Paris. He did. He worked as a translator in UNESCO and lived in an apartment in Joseph Granier Street. He wasn’t interested in politics that much to join one of those Communist parties nor was he much interested in those Hippie cults fashionable in 60’s. In fact he always kept his safety distance with everything. Except for love. He is badly in love with a “bad girl”; a “little girl” from Peru who each time she appeared in his life, she awoke passion and temptation and then regret and sorrow after leaving him alone. What I liked more about this book is that it is not only a historical novel but also it could masterfully cover the cultural, social and sometimes literary movements in Europe, Latin America and also in eastern countries that I think it implies the extensive knowledge and personal experiences of the author.<br /><br /><br />P.S.: <br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I first rated this book for 5 stars then reduced it to 4, because I felt there were sometimes some personal judgments from the author...There are many nationalities in the book, and the narrator seemed to not like some of then, for instance French or why should the most horrible lover of "the bad girl" be a Japanese?? I don't know...maybe I am too obsessive... <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

<div id="review_156366290" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156366290" />
    <a title="Alexandra Turney" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1701729-alexandra-turney"><img alt="Alexandra Turney" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544784987p2/1701729.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156366290?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alexandra Turney" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alexandra Turney" href="/user/show/1701729-alexandra-turney">Alexandra Turney</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1701729-alexandra-turney?shelf=south-america">south-america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156366290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9201648205168423956">So unbelievably misogynistic. If this had been a Victorian novel, I'd still have found it difficult to accept the misogyny, but as this was published in 2006, there really are no excuses...<br/><br/>I found it gripping (despite the fact that the plot is completely predictable) - it's entertaining, at least, despite all its flaws. The chapter set in London was interesting, and I liked some of the minor characters. But the "good boy" and "bad girl", the main characters, are so frustrating and difficult to s</span>
  <span id="freeText9201648205168423956" style="display:none">So unbelievably misogynistic. If this had been a Victorian novel, I'd still have found it difficult to accept the misogyny, but as this was published in 2006, there really are no excuses...<br /><br />I found it gripping (despite the fact that the plot is completely predictable) - it's entertaining, at least, despite all its flaws. The chapter set in London was interesting, and I liked some of the minor characters. But the "good boy" and "bad girl", the main characters, are so frustrating and difficult to sympathise with that the novel just doesn't work overall. The bad girl disappears, turns up in a new disguise, is horrible to Ricardo, they sleep together, he tells her he loves her, she's continues to be horrible, then disappears again for a few years, then reappears...and so on ad infinitum. If the characters were more compelling, the predictability of the plot wouldn't matter so much, but Ricardo is dull, defined only by his attraction to a woman so completely awful and vapid that it's impossible for the reader to sympathise with him. It's not a romantic story. It's thoroughly depressing, shallow, and unrealistic. Vargas Llosa's one attempt to give the bad girl some personality that isn't related to her sexuality (through some sentimental scenes with a mute, adopted child), is embarrassing and unconvincing. Most of the time, however, her existence is only defined by her relationships with the various men who are either infatuated with her, or abusive towards her. Towards the end of the book, it seems as though she's being physically punished for her behaviour by the author himself, and I found the ending particularly distasteful. Ultimately, you learn more about her vagina than you ever do about her personality. I was hoping for a much more fun, imaginative, liberal book, but "The Bad Girl" is old-fashioned, moralistic, and pretty much the opposite of fun...<br /><br />The only other book I've read by Vargas Llosa is "The Notebooks of Don Rigoberto", which I enjoyed, but "The Bad Girl" has put me off reading anything else by him for a while. I know that he's essentially a good writer, but "The Bad Girl" is not a good book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9201648205168423956" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_360539673" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/360539673" />
    <a title="Linda Abhors the New GR Design" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8619142-linda-abhors-the-new-gr-design"><img alt="Linda Abhors the New GR Design" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543189316p2/8619142.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/360539673?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda Abhors the New GR Design" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda Abhors the New GR Design" href="/user/show/8619142-linda-abhors-the-new-gr-design">Linda Abhors the New GR Design</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8619142-linda-abhors-the-new-gr-design?shelf=boston-book-club">boston-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8619142-linda-abhors-the-new-gr-design?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer360539673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9840002153412050196">My impression hasn´t changed since the first reading, so I´ve just skimmed back over the last two hundred pages, and only because my book club chose this one. There´s a bit of Mme Bovary (the obsessive love, and the woman who marries for fortune) and a bit of L´Education Sentimentale (the name Arnoux, for example, being saved financially by the death of a rich aunt/uncle, and running into childhood friends, who are his means to connecting with her in the various locations). But those similaritie</span>
  <span id="freeText9840002153412050196" style="display:none">My impression hasn´t changed since the first reading, so I´ve just skimmed back over the last two hundred pages, and only because my book club chose this one. There´s a bit of Mme Bovary (the obsessive love, and the woman who marries for fortune) and a bit of L´Education Sentimentale (the name Arnoux, for example, being saved financially by the death of a rich aunt/uncle, and running into childhood friends, who are his means to connecting with her in the various locations). But those similarities are limited to the level of plot; there´s none of Flaubert´s rich, exquisite description. Unlike Flaubert, Vargas LLosa limits his perspective to the protagonist, Ricardo, whose obsessive love for Lily-Arlette-etc. never waivers, because he <i>imagines</i> what she's had to go through to get to where she is. We never know what makes Lily/Arlette/etc. tick. And frankly, I don't know that I care. His dream is to live in Paris, even if he has to struggle financially to do so. Her dream is to be rich, regardless of where that takes her (as long as it's not back to Peru) and with whom. Neither seems to recognize that their life dreams simply aren't compatible; it's as if, whenever they run into each other, they revert back to their emotionally immature teen years. Not horrible, just not engaging or very interesting.<br />Vargas Llosa´s works, for me, seem to be ¨love ´em or leave ´em¨; I really love Conversations in the Cathedral but hated ¨The Celt´s Dream¨. This one seems to pull heavily from his youth (my guess, unsubstantiated) so it´s somewhat interesting. But only somewhat...In a world/life with so many books, I don't get to re-read many, and didn't really enjoy re-reading this one. It's just so unremarkable that when re-reading it, I found myself thinking, "I really don't remember too much about this book." In other words, it didn't make an impact then, and it's not really making an impact now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9840002153412050196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_810198753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/810198753" />
    <a title="Gadi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2065771-gadi"><img alt="Gadi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356496436p2/2065771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/810198753?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gadi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gadi" href="/user/show/2065771-gadi">Gadi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer810198753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4003926680133249277">"Tell me some more cheap, sentimental things."<br/><br/>The Bad Girl says this to Ricardito like a hundred times in this book, and each time she does, it becomes more and more eye-rollingly irritating. I mean, the first time it happens I was totally okay with it -- the story begins on a high note and crescendos up for the first 100 pages or so, with the Chilean girls and Miraflores and then the beginning of the Paris section -- all of which engrossed me. The simple, honest writing style smelled authentic </span>
  <span id="freeText4003926680133249277" style="display:none">"Tell me some more cheap, sentimental things."<br /><br />The Bad Girl says this to Ricardito like a hundred times in this book, and each time she does, it becomes more and more eye-rollingly irritating. I mean, the first time it happens I was totally okay with it -- the story begins on a high note and crescendos up for the first 100 pages or so, with the Chilean girls and Miraflores and then the beginning of the Paris section -- all of which engrossed me. The simple, honest writing style smelled authentic and Spanish to me, and Ricardito as the crushing, aimless writer only kept me a sympathetic reader.<br /><br />But then the plotline started to loop like a 70s sitcom. Ricardito has a fling with the Bad Girl, she breaks his heart again because she's an evil fuck with no personality, his best friend leaves him and dies, etc. -- repeat. The only constant (other than the repeating plotline) is Ricardito's blind love for the Bad Girl, that terribly-drawn gold digger. Her dialogue leaves everything to be desired -- as does every other depiction of her. She's not a real person. Llosa makes that clear by christening her as a trope -- "The Bad Girl," the unsympathetic "skinny bitch," conniving and ambitious and mean. <br /><br />We're supposed to feel for poor Ricardito, with his endless stream of cheap, sentimental sayings and his obsessive love for the girl who's given him a life-long case of the blue balls. But once you reduce his life to a constantly looping chain of predictable events, an endless stream of caricatured characters who ebb and flow every 70 pages or so, and an abiding obsession with a shit of an individual, Ricardito becomes the least interesting protagonist ever.<br /><br />After reading this book, I don't understand why Llosa is so renowned. I liked parts of it -- the first ~100 pages, and the last ~30, and some descriptions rang true to my life. But, in addition to the above flaws, Llosa's writing becomes as repetitive as his plot past a certain point. And the way he treats certain things in this book -- the AIDS epidemic, BDSM, women in general -- comes off as careless and reductionist, products of a narrow-minded writer. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4003926680133249277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1331020794" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1331020794" />
    <a title="Joseph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5860412-joseph"><img alt="Joseph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549200708p2/5860412.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1331020794?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joseph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joseph" href="/user/show/5860412-joseph">Joseph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788806190729" href="/book/show/9692867-avventure-della-ragazza-cattiva">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=fiction-in-translation">fiction-in-translation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1331020794" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer695937362470317142">This novel's first-person narrator is one Ricardo Somocurcio, whom we accompany from his teenage years in 1950s Lima, when he dreams of settling down in Paris, throughout later decades when, his dream achieved, he works as an interpreter in the French capital and around the world.<br/><br/>The real protagonist of the story however is, La Nina Mala, the "bad girl" of the title. We first meet her when, as a supposed daughter of Chilean immigrants, she turns heads in Lima. It is here that Ricardo (together w</span>
  <span id="freeText695937362470317142" style="display:none">This novel's first-person narrator is one Ricardo Somocurcio, whom we accompany from his teenage years in 1950s Lima, when he dreams of settling down in Paris, throughout later decades when, his dream achieved, he works as an interpreter in the French capital and around the world.<br /><br />The real protagonist of the story however is, La Nina Mala, the "bad girl" of the title. We first meet her when, as a supposed daughter of Chilean immigrants, she turns heads in Lima. It is here that Ricardo (together with most of his male friends) is first besotted with her. It eventually turns out that she is no less Peruvian than Ricardo and that the background she has made up is simply a ruse to spice up her life story. <br /><br />Indeed, subterfuge is "la nina mala" 's defining trait. She literally changes name and identity as, over the years, she flits in and out of Ricardo's life at the most unexpected of times. Ricardo falls for her again and again, although he is well aware that she is an opportunistic, cynical woman who, in a rare show of honesty, admits that she will never love him. Against the backdrop of a changing society (from stylish Paris to swinging London at the time of the onset of the AIDS crisis, to Tokyo and Spain), we witness, almost voyeur-like, to a relationship which veers wildly between teenage romance, heart-warming love and erotic obsession. <br /><br />Like its main character, this novel does not try to be realistic and, as coincidences pile up, the reader is tempted to project allegorical interpretations onto "la nina mala". The most obvious one is that she is a symbol of a novelist's inspiration - a "Muse" of sorts. A novelist's calling is like a siren-song, or like la nina mala's charm - hard to ignore, even if it brings sacrifice, pain and penury. <br /><br />Whether the novel is read at face value or as a symbolic journey, it remains a poetic work, beautifully rendered in this Italian translation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="695937362470317142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_667317723" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/667317723" />
    <a title="Roxana Chirilă" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21223898-roxana-chiril"><img alt="Roxana Chirilă" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370981378p2/21223898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/667317723?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roxana Chirilă" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roxana Chirilă" href="/user/show/21223898-roxana-chiril">Roxana Chirilă</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789735019723" href="/book/show/7714958-r-t-cirile-fetei-nes-buite">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer667317723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11680247518390692460">When I was in high school, I read Llosa's "The War of the End of the World", which I simply loved (although I don't know whether I'd still love it now; my tastes have changed since then). So, when a friend offered me "The Bad Girl" in 2009, I was quite happy to read more by the same author - but I was so disappointed, nearly 10 years after, I'm still turning away from any book that has Llosa's name on the cover.<br/><br/>"The Bad Girl" is a book about this pixie of a woman. She's wonderful. She's superb. </span>
  <span id="freeText11680247518390692460" style="display:none">When I was in high school, I read Llosa's "The War of the End of the World", which I simply loved (although I don't know whether I'd still love it now; my tastes have changed since then). So, when a friend offered me "The Bad Girl" in 2009, I was quite happy to read more by the same author - but I was so disappointed, nearly 10 years after, I'm still turning away from any book that has Llosa's name on the cover.<br /><br />"The Bad Girl" is a book about this pixie of a woman. She's wonderful. She's superb. She's beautiful. She lies, lies, lies and makes up new histories and names for herself. She's charming and wonderful and she lives life at one hundred miles per hour. <br /><br /><blockquote>“Well, at heart I knew she'd never be a normal woman. And I didn't want her to be one, because what I loved in her were the indomitable and unpredictable aspects of her personality”</blockquote><br /><br />That would be fine, if we got to know her, but her only role in this is to be a male fantasy. She's the impossible woman, always slipping between your fingers, taking and never giving, mysterious and sexy. Ricardo, the main character, is an ordinary boy who falls desperately in love with her when he's fifteen, and who encounters her every ten or so years as he goes on with his life - he meets her as a trophy wife, the mistress of a perverted Japanese yakuza man, and finally a broken woman, but he loves-loves-loves her.<br /><br />I disliked Ricardo, who never moves on and who always seems to be waiting for her, while carrying on with a perfectly boring life that seems to offer him no satisfaction. I disliked her, as well - just like Ricardo, she doesn't have much of a personality, even though she looks shiny from a distance.<br /><br />And the plot... well, there isn't much of one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11680247518390692460" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
